	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Diocletian</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the band, see <a href="/wiki/Diocletian_(band)" title="Diocletian (band)">Diocletian (band)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Augustus of the Eastern Roman Empire</div>

<p><b>Diocletian</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/aɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">aɪ</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="/ʃ/: &#39;sh&#39; in &#39;shy&#39;">ʃ</span><span title="/ən/: &#39;on&#39; in &#39;button&#39;">ən</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Gaius Aurelius Valerius Diocletianus Augustus</i>), born <b>Diocles</b> (22 December 244 – 3 December 311),<sup id="cite_ref-DEATH_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DEATH-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> was a <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a> from 284 to 305. Born to a family of low status in <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia_(Roman_province)" title="Dalmatia (Roman province)">Dalmatia</a>, Diocletian rose through the ranks of the military to become <a href="/wiki/Roman_cavalry" title="Roman cavalry">Roman cavalry</a> commander to the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Carus" title="Carus">Carus</a>. After the deaths of Carus and his son <a href="/wiki/Numerian" title="Numerian">Numerian</a> on campaign in Persia, Diocletian was proclaimed emperor. The title was also claimed by Carus' surviving son, <a href="/wiki/Carinus" title="Carinus">Carinus</a>, but Diocletian defeated him in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Margus" title="Battle of the Margus">Battle of the Margus</a>. 
</p><p>Diocletian's reign stabilized the empire and marks the end of the <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century" title="Crisis of the Third Century">Crisis of the Third Century</a>. He appointed fellow officer <a href="/wiki/Maximian" title="Maximian">Maximian</a> as <a href="/wiki/Augustus_(honorific)" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustus (honorific)">Augustus</a>, co-emperor, in 286. Diocletian reigned in the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Eastern Empire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maximian" title="Maximian">Maximian</a> reigned in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Empire</a>. Diocletian delegated further on 1 March 293, appointing <a href="/wiki/Galerius" title="Galerius">Galerius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constantius_Chlorus" title="Constantius Chlorus">Constantius</a> as <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">Caesars</a>, junior co-emperors, under himself and Maximian respectively. Under this '<a href="/wiki/Tetrarchy" title="Tetrarchy">tetrarchy</a>', or "rule of four", each emperor would rule over a quarter-division of the empire. Diocletian secured the empire's borders and purged it of all threats to his power. He defeated the <a href="/wiki/Sarmatia" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarmatia">Sarmatians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carpi_(people)" title="Carpi (people)">Carpi</a> during several campaigns between 285 and 299, the <a href="/wiki/Alamanni" class="mw-redirect" title="Alamanni">Alamanni</a> in 288, and usurpers in <a href="/wiki/Egypt_(Roman_province)" title="Egypt (Roman province)">Egypt</a> between 297 and 298. Galerius, aided by Diocletian, campaigned successfully against <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sassanid Persia</a>, the empire's traditional enemy. In 299 he sacked their capital, <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a>. Diocletian led the subsequent negotiations and achieved a lasting and favourable peace.
</p><p>Diocletian separated and enlarged the empire's civil and military services and reorganized the empire's provincial divisions, establishing the largest and most <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucratic</a> government in the history of the empire. He established new administrative centres in <a href="/wiki/Nicomedia" title="Nicomedia">Nicomedia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mediolanum" title="Mediolanum">Mediolanum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sirmium" title="Sirmium">Sirmium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trevorum</a>, closer to the empire's frontiers than the traditional capital at Rome. Building on third-century trends towards <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">absolutism</a>, he styled himself an autocrat, elevating himself above the empire's masses with imposing forms of court ceremonies and architecture. Bureaucratic and military growth, constant campaigning, and construction projects increased the state's expenditures and necessitated a comprehensive tax reform. From at least 297 on, imperial taxation was standardized, made more equitable, and levied at generally higher rates.
</p><p>Not all of Diocletian's plans were successful: the <a href="/wiki/Edict_on_Maximum_Prices" title="Edict on Maximum Prices">Edict on Maximum Prices</a> (301), his attempt to curb <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> via <a href="/wiki/Price_controls" title="Price controls">price controls</a>, was counterproductive and quickly ignored. Although effective while he ruled, Diocletian's <a href="/wiki/Civil_wars_of_the_Tetrarchy" title="Civil wars of the Tetrarchy">tetrarchic system collapsed</a> after his abdication under the competing dynastic claims of <a href="/wiki/Maxentius" title="Maxentius">Maxentius</a> and Constantine, sons of Maximian and Constantius respectively. The <a href="/wiki/Diocletianic_Persecution" title="Diocletianic Persecution">Diocletianic Persecution</a> (303–312), the empire's last, largest, and bloodiest official <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Persecution of Christianity">persecution of Christianity</a>, failed to eliminate Christianity in the empire; indeed, after 324, Christianity became the empire's preferred religion under <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine</a>. Despite these failures and challenges, Diocletian's reforms fundamentally changed the structure of Roman imperial government and helped stabilize the empire economically and militarily, enabling the empire to remain essentially intact for another 150 years despite being near the brink of collapse in Diocletian's youth. Weakened by illness, Diocletian left the imperial office on 1 May 305, and became the first Roman emperor to abdicate the position voluntarily. He lived out his retirement in <a href="/wiki/Diocletian%27s_Palace" title="Diocletian&#39;s Palace">his palace on the Dalmatian coast</a>, tending to his vegetable gardens. His palace eventually became the core of the modern-day city of <a href="/wiki/Split,_Croatia" title="Split, Croatia">Split</a> in <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Religious_persecutions"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Religious persecutions</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Salona_-_amphitheatre_-_panorama.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Salona_-_amphitheatre_-_panorama.jpg/500px-Salona_-_amphitheatre_-_panorama.jpg" decoding="async" width="500" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Salona_-_amphitheatre_-_panorama.jpg/750px-Salona_-_amphitheatre_-_panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Salona_-_amphitheatre_-_panorama.jpg/1000px-Salona_-_amphitheatre_-_panorama.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4199" data-file-height="1507" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Salona_-_amphitheatre_-_panorama.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Panorama of amphitheatre in <a href="/wiki/Salona" title="Salona">Salona</a></div></div></div>
<p>Diocletian was born near <a href="/wiki/Salona" title="Salona">Salona</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia_(Roman_province)" title="Dalmatia (Roman province)">Dalmatia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Solin,_Croatia" class="mw-redirect" title="Solin, Croatia">Solin</a> in modern <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>), some time around 244.<sup id="cite_ref-BIRTH_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BIRTH-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> His parents gave him the Greek name Diocles, or possibly Diocles Valerius.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The modern historian <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Barnes" title="Timothy Barnes">Timothy Barnes</a> takes his official birthday, 22 December, as his actual birthdate. Other historians are not so certain.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> His parents were of low status; <a href="/wiki/Eutropius_(historian)" title="Eutropius (historian)">Eutropius</a> records "that he is said by most writers to have been the son of a scribe, but by some to have been a freedman of a senator called Anulinus." The first forty years of his life are mostly obscure.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> chronicler <a href="/wiki/Joannes_Zonaras" title="Joannes Zonaras">Joannes Zonaras</a> states that he was <i><a href="/wiki/Dux" title="Dux">Dux</a> <a href="/wiki/Moesia" title="Moesia">Moesiae</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> a commander of forces on the lower <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The often-unreliable <i><a href="/wiki/Augustan_History" title="Augustan History">Historia Augusta</a></i> states that he served in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Gaul" title="Roman Gaul">Gaul</a>, but this account is not corroborated by other sources and is ignored by modern historians of the period.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The first time Diocletian's whereabouts are accurately established, in 282, the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Carus" title="Carus">Carus</a> made him commander of the <i><a href="/wiki/Protectores_domestici" class="mw-redirect" title="Protectores domestici">Protectores domestici</a></i>, the elite cavalry force directly attached to the Imperial household – a post that earned him the honour of a consulship in 283.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> As such, he took part in Carus' subsequent Persian campaign.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_of_Numerian">Death of Numerian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Death of Numerian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carus's death, amid a successful war with Persia and in mysterious circumstances<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> – he was believed to have been struck by lightning or killed by Persian soldiers<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> – left his sons Numerian and Carinus as the new <i>Augusti</i>. Carinus quickly made his way to Rome from his post in Gaul as imperial commissioner and arrived there by January 284, becoming legitimate Emperor in the West. Numerian lingered in the East.<sup id="cite_ref-BNSCE4_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNSCE4-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The Roman withdrawal from Persia was orderly and unopposed.<sup id="cite_ref-SSC133_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSC133-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sassanid" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid">Sassanid</a> king <a href="/wiki/Bahram_II" title="Bahram II">Bahram II</a> could not field an army against them as he was still struggling to establish his authority. By March 284, Numerian had only reached <a href="/wiki/Homs" title="Homs">Emesa (Homs)</a> in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>; by November, only Asia Minor.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In Emesa he was apparently still alive and in good health: he issued the only extant <a href="/wiki/Rescript" title="Rescript">rescript</a> in his name there,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;Note 1&#93;</a></sup> but after he left the city, his staff, including the prefect (Numerian's father-in-law, and as such the dominant influence in the Emperor's entourage)<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Flavius_Aper" title="Lucius Flavius Aper">Aper</a>, reported that he suffered from an inflammation of the eyes. He travelled in a closed coach from then on.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> When the army reached <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-BNSCE4_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNSCE4-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> some of the soldiers smelled an odor emanating from the coach.<sup id="cite_ref-SSC133_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSC133-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> They opened its curtains and inside they found Numerian dead.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Both <a href="/wiki/Eutropius_(historian)" title="Eutropius (historian)">Eutropius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aurelius_Victor" title="Aurelius Victor">Aurelius Victor</a> describe Numerian's death as an assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Aper officially broke the news in <a href="/wiki/Nicomedia" title="Nicomedia">Nicomedia</a> (<a href="/wiki/%C4%B0zmit" title="İzmit">İzmit</a>) in November.<sup id="cite_ref-P280_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P280-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Numerianus' generals and tribunes called a council for the succession, and chose Diocles as Emperor,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> in spite of Aper's attempts to garner support.<sup id="cite_ref-P280_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P280-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> On 20 November 284, the army of the east gathered on a hill 5 kilometres (3.1&#160;mi) outside Nicomedia. The army unanimously saluted Diocles as their new Augustus, and he accepted the purple imperial vestments. He raised his sword to the light of the sun and swore an oath disclaiming responsibility for Numerian's death. He asserted that Aper had killed Numerian and concealed it.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In full view of the army, Diocles drew his sword and killed Aper.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> According to the <i>Historia Augusta</i>, he quoted from <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> while doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Soon after Aper's death, Diocles changed his name to the more Latinate "Diocletianus"<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>--in full, Gaius Aurelius Valerius Diocletianus.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict_with_Carinus">Conflict with Carinus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Conflict with Carinus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Montemartini_-_Carino_1030439.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Montemartini_-_Carino_1030439.JPG/170px-Montemartini_-_Carino_1030439.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Montemartini_-_Carino_1030439.JPG/255px-Montemartini_-_Carino_1030439.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Montemartini_-_Carino_1030439.JPG/340px-Montemartini_-_Carino_1030439.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1026" data-file-height="1378" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Montemartini_-_Carino_1030439.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Head of <a href="/wiki/Carinus" title="Carinus">Carinus</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Centrale_Montemartini" class="mw-redirect" title="Centrale Montemartini">Centrale Montemartini</a></div></div></div>
<p>After his accession, Diocletian and Lucius Caesonius Bassus<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> were named as consuls and assumed the <i><a href="/wiki/Fasces" title="Fasces">fasces</a></i> in place of Carinus and Numerianus.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Bassus was a member of a <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">senatorial</a> family from <a href="/wiki/Campania" title="Campania">Campania</a>, a former <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consul</a> and proconsul of Africa, chosen by Probus for signal distinction.<sup id="cite_ref-BNSCE5_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNSCE5-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> He was skilled in areas of government where Diocletian presumably had no experience.<sup id="cite_ref-P280_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P280-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian's elevation of Bassus as consul symbolized his rejection of Carinus' government in Rome, his refusal to accept second-tier status to any other emperor,<sup id="cite_ref-BNSCE5_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNSCE5-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and his willingness to continue the long-standing collaboration between the empire's senatorial and military aristocracies.<sup id="cite_ref-P280_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P280-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> It also tied his success to that of the Senate, whose support he would need in his advance on Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-BNSCE5_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNSCE5-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diocletian was not the only challenger to Carinus' rule; the usurper <a href="/wiki/Sabinus_Julianus" title="Sabinus Julianus">M. Aurelius Julianus</a>, Carinus' <i>corrector Venetiae</i>, took control of northern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pannonia" title="Pannonia">Pannonia</a> after Diocletian's accession.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Julianus minted coins from the mint at Siscia (<a href="/wiki/Sisak" title="Sisak">Sisak</a>, Croatia) declaring himself emperor and promising freedom. It was all good publicity for Diocletian, and it aided in his portrayal of Carinus as a cruel and oppressive tyrant.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Julianus' forces were weak, however, and were handily dispersed when Carinus' armies moved from Britain to northern Italy. As leader of the united East, Diocletian was clearly the greater threat.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Over the winter of 284–85, Diocletian advanced west across the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>. In the spring, some time before the end of May,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> his armies met Carinus' across the river Margus (<a href="/wiki/Great_Morava" title="Great Morava">Great Morava</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Moesia" title="Moesia">Moesia</a>. In modern accounts, the site has been located between the Mons Aureus (Seone, west of <a href="/wiki/Smederevo" title="Smederevo">Smederevo</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Viminacium" title="Viminacium">Viminacium</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-BNSCE5_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNSCE5-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> near modern <a href="/wiki/Belgrade" title="Belgrade">Belgrade</a>, Serbia.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite having the stronger, more powerful army, Carinus held the weaker position. His rule was unpopular, and it was later alleged that he had mistreated the Senate and seduced his officers' wives.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> It is possible that <a href="/wiki/Constantius_Chlorus" title="Constantius Chlorus">Flavius Constantius</a>, the governor of Dalmatia and Diocletian's associate in the household guard, had already defected to Diocletian in the early spring.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Margus" title="Battle of the Margus">Battle of the Margus</a> began, Carinus' prefect <a href="/wiki/Titus_Claudius_Aurelius_Aristobulus" title="Titus Claudius Aurelius Aristobulus">Aristobulus</a> also defected.<sup id="cite_ref-P280_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P280-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In the course of the battle, Carinus was killed by his own men. Following Diocletian's victory, both the western and the eastern armies acclaimed him as Emperor.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian exacted an oath of allegiance from the defeated army and departed for Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_rule">Early rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diocletian_RIC_106_Cyzicus_obv.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Diocletian_RIC_106_Cyzicus_obv.jpg/170px-Diocletian_RIC_106_Cyzicus_obv.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Diocletian_RIC_106_Cyzicus_obv.jpg/255px-Diocletian_RIC_106_Cyzicus_obv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f1/Diocletian_RIC_106_Cyzicus_obv.jpg 2x" data-file-width="273" data-file-height="261" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diocletian_RIC_106_Cyzicus_obv.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Antoninianus" title="Antoninianus">Antoninianus</a> of Diocletian</div></div></div>
<p>Diocletian may have become involved in battles against the <a href="/wiki/Quadi" title="Quadi">Quadi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcomanni" title="Marcomanni">Marcomanni</a> immediately after the Battle of the Margus. He eventually made his way to northern Italy and made an imperial government, but it is not known whether he visited the city of Rome at this time.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> There is a contemporary issue of coins suggestive of an imperial <i><a href="/wiki/Adventus_(ceremony)" title="Adventus (ceremony)">adventus</a></i> (arrival) for the city,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> but some modern historians state that Diocletian avoided the city, and that he did so on principle, as the city and its Senate were no longer politically relevant to the affairs of the empire and needed to be taught as much. Diocletian dated his reign from his elevation by the army, not the date of his ratification by the Senate,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> following the practice established by Carus, who had declared the Senate's ratification a useless formality.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> However, Diocletian was to offer proof of his deference towards the Senate by retaining Aristobulus as ordinary consul and colleague for 285 (one of the few instances during the Late Empire in which an emperor admitted a <i>privatus</i> as his colleague)<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and by creating senior senators Vettius Aquilinus and Junius Maximus ordinary consuls for the following year – for Maximus, it was his second consulship.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bust_of_Diocletian_at_the_National_Museum_of_Serbia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Bust_of_Diocletian_at_the_National_Museum_of_Serbia.jpg/220px-Bust_of_Diocletian_at_the_National_Museum_of_Serbia.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="391" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Bust_of_Diocletian_at_the_National_Museum_of_Serbia.jpg/330px-Bust_of_Diocletian_at_the_National_Museum_of_Serbia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Bust_of_Diocletian_at_the_National_Museum_of_Serbia.jpg/440px-Bust_of_Diocletian_at_the_National_Museum_of_Serbia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bust_of_Diocletian_at_the_National_Museum_of_Serbia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Head of Diocletian at the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Serbia" title="National Museum of Serbia">National Museum of Serbia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Nevertheless, if Diocletian ever did enter Rome shortly after his accession, he did not stay long;<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> he is attested back in the Balkans by 2 November 285, on campaign against the <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diocletian replaced the <a href="/wiki/Praefectus_urbis" class="mw-redirect" title="Praefectus urbis">prefect</a> of Rome with his consular colleague Bassus. Most officials who had served under Carinus, however, retained their offices under Diocletian.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In an act of <i>clementia</i> denoted by the epitomator <a href="/wiki/Aurelius_Victor" title="Aurelius Victor">Aurelius Victor</a> as unusual,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian did not kill or depose Carinus' traitorous praetorian prefect and consul Ti. Claudius Aurelius Aristobulus, but confirmed him in both roles.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> He later gave him the proconsulate of Africa and the post of urban prefect for 295.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The other figures who retained their offices might have also betrayed Carinus.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maximian_made_co-emperor">Maximian made co-emperor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Maximian made co-emperor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IMP_MAXIMIANVS_P_AVG.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/IMP_MAXIMIANVS_P_AVG.gif/220px-IMP_MAXIMIANVS_P_AVG.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/IMP_MAXIMIANVS_P_AVG.gif/330px-IMP_MAXIMIANVS_P_AVG.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/78/IMP_MAXIMIANVS_P_AVG.gif 2x" data-file-width="391" data-file-height="396" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IMP_MAXIMIANVS_P_AVG.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Maximian's consistent loyalty to Diocletian proved an important component of the tetrarchy's early successes.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The assassinations of <a href="/wiki/Aurelian" title="Aurelian">Aurelian</a> and Probus demonstrated that sole rulership was dangerous to the stability of the empire.<sup id="cite_ref-P280_26-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P280-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Conflict boiled in every province, from Gaul to Syria, Egypt to the lower Danube. It was too much for one person to control, and Diocletian needed a lieutenant.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> At some time in 285 at <a href="/wiki/Mediolanum" title="Mediolanum">Mediolanum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;Note 2&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian raised his fellow-officer <a href="/wiki/Maximian" title="Maximian">Maximian</a> to the office of <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">caesar</a>, making him co-emperor.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of dual rulership was nothing new to the Roman Empire. <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>, the first Emperor, had nominally shared power with his colleagues, and more formal offices of Co-Emperor had existed from <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a> onward.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Most recently, Emperor Carus and his sons had ruled together, albeit unsuccessfully. Diocletian was in a less comfortable position than most of his predecessors, as he had a daughter, Valeria, but no sons. His co-ruler had to be from outside his family, raising the question of trust.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Some historians state that Diocletian adopted Maximian as his <i>filius Augusti</i>, his "Augustan son", upon his appointment to the throne, following the precedent of some previous Emperors.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> This argument has not been universally accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The relationship between Diocletian and Maximian was quickly couched in religious terms. Around 287 Diocletian assumed the title <i>Iovius</i>, and Maximian assumed the title <i>Herculius</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The titles were probably meant to convey certain characteristics of their associated leaders. Diocletian, in <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jovian</a> style, would take on the dominating roles of planning and commanding; Maximian, in <a href="/wiki/Hercules" title="Hercules">Herculian</a> mode, would act as Jupiter's <a href="/wiki/Hero" title="Hero">heroic</a> subordinate.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> For all their religious connotations, the emperors were not "gods" in the tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_cult_(ancient_Rome)" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial cult (ancient Rome)">Imperial cult</a>—although they may have been hailed as such in Imperial <a href="/wiki/Panegyric" title="Panegyric">panegyrics</a>. Instead, they were seen as the gods' representatives, effecting their will on earth.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The shift from military acclamation to divine sanctification took the power to appoint emperors away from the army. Religious legitimization elevated Diocletian and Maximian above potential rivals in a way military power and dynastic claims could not.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict_with_Sarmatia_and_Persia">Conflict with Sarmatia and Persia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Conflict with Sarmatia and Persia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After his acclamation, Maximian was dispatched to fight the rebel <a href="/wiki/Bagaudae" title="Bagaudae">Bagaudae</a>, insurgent peasants of Gaul. Diocletian returned to the East, progressing slowly.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> By 2 November, he had only reached Civitas Iovia (Botivo, near <a href="/wiki/Ptuj" title="Ptuj">Ptuj</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In the Balkans during the autumn of 285, he encountered a tribe of <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a> who demanded assistance. The Sarmatians requested that Diocletian either help them recover their lost lands or grant them pasturage rights within the empire. Diocletian refused and fought a battle with them, but was unable to secure a complete victory. The nomadic pressures of the <a href="/wiki/European_Plain" title="European Plain">European Plain</a> remained and could not be solved by a single war; soon the Sarmatians would have to be fought again.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diocletian wintered in <a href="/wiki/Nicomedia" title="Nicomedia">Nicomedia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;Note 3&#93;</a></sup> There may have been a revolt in the eastern provinces at this time, as he brought settlers from <a href="/wiki/Asia_(Roman_province)" title="Asia (Roman province)">Asia</a> to populate emptied farmlands in <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> He visited <a href="/wiki/Syria_Palaestina" title="Syria Palaestina">Syria Palaestina</a> the following spring, <sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;Note 4&#93;</a></sup> His stay in the East saw diplomatic success in the conflict with Persia: in 287, <a href="/wiki/Bahram_II" title="Bahram II">Bahram II</a> granted him precious gifts, declared open friendship with the Empire, and invited Diocletian to visit him.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Roman sources insist that the act was entirely voluntary.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Around the same time, perhaps in 287,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Persia relinquished claims on <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and recognized Roman authority over territory to the west and south of the Tigris. The western portion of Armenia was incorporated into the empire and made a province. <a href="/wiki/Tiridates_III_of_Armenia" title="Tiridates III of Armenia">Tiridates III</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsacid_dynasty_of_Armenia" title="Arsacid dynasty of Armenia">Arsacid</a> claimant to the Armenian throne and Roman client, had been disinherited and forced to take refuge in the empire after the Persian conquest of 252–53. In 287, he returned to lay claim to the eastern half of his ancestral domain and encountered no opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Bahram II's gifts were widely recognized as symbolic of a victory in the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Roman-Persian_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman-Persian Wars">conflict with Persia</a>, and Diocletian was hailed as the "founder of eternal peace". The events might have represented a formal end to Carus' eastern campaign, which probably ended without an acknowledged peace.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> At the conclusion of discussions with the Persians, Diocletian re-organized the Mesopotamian frontier and fortified the city of <a href="/wiki/Circesium" title="Circesium">Circesium</a> (Buseire, Syria) on the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maximian_made_Augustus">Maximian made Augustus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Maximian made Augustus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Maximian's campaigns were not proceeding as smoothly. The Bagaudae had been easily suppressed, but <a href="/wiki/Carausius" title="Carausius">Carausius</a>, the man he had put in charge of operations against <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Frankish</a> <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">pirates</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Saxon_Shore" title="Saxon Shore">Saxon Shore</a>, had, according to literary sources, begun keeping the goods seized from the pirates for himself. Maximian issued a death warrant for his larcenous subordinate. Carausius fled the Continent, proclaimed himself Augustus, and agitated Britain and northwestern Gaul into open revolt against Maximian and Diocletian.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Far more probable, according to the archaeological evidence available, is that Carausius probably had held some important military post in Britain<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and had already a firm basis of power in both Britain and Northern Gaul (a coin hoard found in <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a> proves that he was in control of that mainland area at the beginning of his rebellion) and that he profited from the lack of legitimacy of the central government.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Carausius strove to have his legitimacy as a junior emperor acknowledged by Diocletian: in his coinage (of far better quality than the official one, especially his silver pieces) he extolled the "concord" between him and the central power (PAX AVGGG, "the Peace of the three Augusti", read one bronze piece from 290, displaying, on the other side, Carausius together with Diocletian and Maximian, with the caption CARAVSIVS ET FRATRES SVI, "Carausius &amp; his brothers" <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.dot-domesday.me.uk/empires2.htm">[1]</a> ).<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> However, Diocletian could not allow elbow room to a breakaway regional usurper following in <a href="/wiki/Postumus" title="Postumus">Postumus</a>'s footprints to enter, solely of his own accord, the imperial college.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> So Carausius had to go.
</p><p>Spurred by the crisis, on 1 April 286,<sup id="cite_ref-AUGGGG_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AUGGGG-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;Note 5&#93;</a></sup> Maximian took up the title of Augustus.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> His appointment is unusual in that it was impossible for Diocletian to have been present to witness the event. It has even been suggested that Maximian usurped the title and was only later recognized by Diocletian in hopes of avoiding civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> This suggestion is unpopular, as it is clear that Diocletian meant for Maximian to act with a certain amount of independence.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> It may be posited, however, that Diocletian felt the need to bind Maximian closer to him, by making him his empowered associate, in order to avoid the possibility of having him striking some sort of deal with Carausius.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoninianus_Carausius_leg4-RIC_0069v.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Antoninianus_Carausius_leg4-RIC_0069v.jpg/220px-Antoninianus_Carausius_leg4-RIC_0069v.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Antoninianus_Carausius_leg4-RIC_0069v.jpg/330px-Antoninianus_Carausius_leg4-RIC_0069v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Antoninianus_Carausius_leg4-RIC_0069v.jpg/440px-Antoninianus_Carausius_leg4-RIC_0069v.jpg 2x" data-file-width="446" data-file-height="206" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoninianus_Carausius_leg4-RIC_0069v.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carausius, rebel emperor of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>. Most of the evidence for Carausius' reign comes from his coinage, which was of generally fine quality.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Maximian realized that he could not immediately suppress the rogue commander, so in 287 he campaigned solely against tribes beyond the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> instead.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> As Carausius was allied to the Franks, Maximian's campaigns could be seen as an effort to deny the separatist emperor in Britain a basis of support on the mainland.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The following spring, as Maximian prepared a fleet for an expedition against Carausius, Diocletian returned from the East to meet Maximian. The two emperors agreed on a joint campaign against the <a href="/wiki/Alamanni" class="mw-redirect" title="Alamanni">Alamanni</a>. Diocletian invaded Germania through Raetia while Maximian progressed from Mainz. Each emperor burned crops and food supplies as he went, destroying the Germans' means of sustenance.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The two men added territory to the empire and allowed Maximian to continue preparations against Carausius without further disturbance.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> On his return to the East, Diocletian managed what was probably another rapid campaign against the resurgent Sarmatians. No details survive, but surviving inscriptions indicate that Diocletian took the title <i>Sarmaticus Maximus</i> after 289.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the East, Diocletian engaged in diplomacy with desert tribes in the regions between Rome and Persia. He might have been attempting to persuade them to ally themselves with Rome, thus reviving the old, Rome-friendly, <a href="/wiki/Palmyra" title="Palmyra">Palmyrene</a> <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">sphere of influence</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ubdeze_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ubdeze-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> or simply attempting to reduce the frequency of their incursions.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> No details survive for these events.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Some of the princes of these states were Persian client kings, a disturbing fact in light of increasing tensions with the Sassanids.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In the West, Maximian lost the fleet built in 288 and 289, probably in the early spring of 290. The <a href="/wiki/Panegyric" title="Panegyric">panegyrist</a> who refers to the loss suggests that its cause was a storm,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> but this might simply have been an attempt to conceal an embarrassing military defeat.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian broke off his tour of the Eastern provinces soon thereafter. He returned with haste to the West, reaching Emesa by 10 May 290,<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> and Sirmium on the Danube by 1 July 290.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diocletian met Maximian in Milan in the winter of 290–91, either in late December 290 or January 291.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The meeting was undertaken with a sense of solemn pageantry. The emperors spent most of their time in public appearances. It has been surmised that the ceremonies were arranged to demonstrate Diocletian's continuing support for his faltering colleague.<sup id="cite_ref-ubdeze_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ubdeze-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> A deputation from the Roman Senate met with the emperors, renewing its infrequent contact with the Imperial office.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The choice of Milan over Rome further snubbed the capital's pride. But then it was already a long established practice that Rome itself was only a ceremonial capital, as the actual seat of the Imperial administration was determined by the needs of defense. Long before Diocletian, <a href="/wiki/Gallienus" title="Gallienus">Gallienus</a> (r. 253–68) had chosen Milan as the seat of his headquarters.<sup id="cite_ref-Elsner,_Imperial_Rome,_73_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elsner,_Imperial_Rome,_73-117">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> If the panegyric detailing the ceremony implied that the true center of the empire was not Rome, but where the emperor sat ("...the capital of the empire appeared to be there, where the two emperors met"),<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> it simply echoed what had already been stated by the historian <a href="/wiki/Herodian" title="Herodian">Herodian</a> in the early third century: "Rome is where the emperor is".<sup id="cite_ref-Elsner,_Imperial_Rome,_73_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elsner,_Imperial_Rome,_73-117">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> During the meeting, decisions on matters of politics and war were probably made in secret.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The Augusti would not meet again until 303.<sup id="cite_ref-ubdeze_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ubdeze-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tetrarchy">Tetrarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Tetrarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tetrarchy" title="Tetrarchy">Tetrarchy</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation_of_the_Tetrarchy">Foundation of the Tetrarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Foundation of the Tetrarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tetrarchy_map3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Tetrarchy_map3.jpg/220px-Tetrarchy_map3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Tetrarchy_map3.jpg/330px-Tetrarchy_map3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Tetrarchy_map3.jpg/440px-Tetrarchy_map3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="991" data-file-height="751" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tetrarchy_map3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Roman Empire under the tetrarchy, showing the <a href="/wiki/Roman_diocese" title="Roman diocese">dioceses</a> and the four tetrarchs' zones of influence post-299, after Diocletian and Galerius had exchanged their allocated provinces.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sbeitla_10.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Sbeitla_10.jpg/220px-Sbeitla_10.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Sbeitla_10.jpg/330px-Sbeitla_10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Sbeitla_10.jpg/440px-Sbeitla_10.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="1712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sbeitla_10.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Triumphal arch of the tetrarchy, <a href="/wiki/Sbeitla" title="Sbeitla">Sbeitla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Some time after his return, and before 293, Diocletian transferred command of the war against Carausius from Maximian to <a href="/wiki/Constantius_Chlorus" title="Constantius Chlorus">Flavius Constantius</a>, a former Governor of Dalmatia and a man of military experience stretching back to <a href="/wiki/Aurelian" title="Aurelian">Aurelian</a>'s campaigns against <a href="/wiki/Zenobia" title="Zenobia">Zenobia</a> (272–73). He was Maximian's praetorian prefect in Gaul, and the husband to Maximian's daughter, <a href="/wiki/Flavia_Maximiana_Theodora" title="Flavia Maximiana Theodora">Theodora</a>. On 1 March 293 at Milan, Maximian gave Constantius the office of caesar.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In the spring of 293, in either Philippopolis (<a href="/wiki/Plovdiv" title="Plovdiv">Plovdiv</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>) or Sirmium, Diocletian would do the same for <a href="/wiki/Galerius" title="Galerius">Galerius</a>, husband to Diocletian's daughter Valeria, and perhaps Diocletian's Praetorian Prefect.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;Note 6&#93;</a></sup> Constantius was assigned Gaul and Britain. Galerius was initially assigned Syria, Palestine, Egypt, and responsibility for the eastern borderlands.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This arrangement is called the tetrarchy, from a <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> term meaning "rulership by four".<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The Tetrarchic Emperors were more or less sovereign in their own lands, and they travelled with their own imperial courts, administrators, secretaries, and armies.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> They were joined by blood and marriage; Diocletian and Maximian now styled themselves as brothers. The senior Co-Emperors formally adopted Galerius and Constantius as sons in 293. These relationships implied a line of succession. Galerius and Constantius would become Augusti after the departure of Diocletian and Maximian. Maximian's son <a href="/wiki/Maxentius" title="Maxentius">Maxentius</a> and Constantius' son <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine</a> would then become Caesars. In preparation for their future roles, Constantine and Maxentius were taken to Diocletian's court in Nicomedia.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Demise_of_Carausius.27_breakaway_Roman_Empire"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Demise_of_Carausius'_breakaway_Roman_Empire">Demise of Carausius' breakaway Roman Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Demise of Carausius&#039; breakaway Roman Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Just before his creation as Caesar, Constantius proceeded to cut Carausius from his base of support in Gaul, recovering Boulogne after a hotly fought siege, a success that would result in Carausius being murdered and replaced by his aide <a href="/wiki/Allectus" title="Allectus">Allectus</a>, who would hold out in his Britain stronghold for a further three years<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> until a two-pronged naval invasion resulted in Allectus' defeat and death at the hands of Constantius' praetorian prefect <a href="/wiki/Julius_Asclepiodotus" title="Julius Asclepiodotus">Julius Asclepiodotus</a>, during a land battle somewhere near <a href="/wiki/Farnham" title="Farnham">Farnham</a>. Constantius himself, after disembarking in the south east, delivered London from a looting party of Frankish deserters in Allectus' pay, something that allowed him to assume the role of liberator of Britain. A famous commemorative medallion depicts a personification of London supplying the victorious Constantius on horseback in which he describes himself as <i>redditor lucis aeternae</i>, 'restorer of the eternal light (<i>viz.</i>, of Rome).'<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The suppression of this threat to the Tetrarchs' legitimacy allowed both Constantius and Maximian to concentrate on outside threats: by 297 Constantius was back on the Rhine and Maximian engaged in a full-scale African campaign against Frankish pirates and nomads, eventually making a triumphal entry into <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> on 10 March 298.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> However, Maximian's failure to deal with Carausius and Allectus on his own had jeopardized the position of Maxentius as putative heir to his father's post as Augustus of the West, with Constantius' son Constantine appearing as a rival claimant.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict_in_the_Balkans_and_Egypt">Conflict in the Balkans and Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Conflict in the Balkans and Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Philae,_Trajan%27s_Kiosk,_Aswan,_Egypt,_Oct_2004.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Philae%2C_Trajan%27s_Kiosk%2C_Aswan%2C_Egypt%2C_Oct_2004.jpg/220px-Philae%2C_Trajan%27s_Kiosk%2C_Aswan%2C_Egypt%2C_Oct_2004.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Philae%2C_Trajan%27s_Kiosk%2C_Aswan%2C_Egypt%2C_Oct_2004.jpg/330px-Philae%2C_Trajan%27s_Kiosk%2C_Aswan%2C_Egypt%2C_Oct_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Philae%2C_Trajan%27s_Kiosk%2C_Aswan%2C_Egypt%2C_Oct_2004.jpg/440px-Philae%2C_Trajan%27s_Kiosk%2C_Aswan%2C_Egypt%2C_Oct_2004.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1175" data-file-height="713" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Philae,_Trajan%27s_Kiosk,_Aswan,_Egypt,_Oct_2004.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajanic</a> temple on the island of <a href="/wiki/Philae" title="Philae">Philae</a>, the newly established border between the <a href="/wiki/Nobatae" class="mw-redirect" title="Nobatae">Nobatae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blemmyes" title="Blemmyes">Blemmyes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a><sup id="cite_ref-BNCE1718_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNCE1718-131">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Diocletian spent the spring of 293 travelling with Galerius from Sirmium (<a href="/wiki/Sremska_Mitrovica" title="Sremska Mitrovica">Sremska Mitrovica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>) to <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a> (<a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>). Diocletian then returned to Sirmium, where he would remain for the following winter and spring. He campaigned against the Sarmatians again in 294, probably in the autumn,<sup id="cite_ref-pymwoj_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pymwoj-132">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> and won a victory against them. The Sarmatians' defeat kept them from the Danube provinces for a long time. Meanwhile, Diocletian built forts north of the Danube,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> at <a href="/wiki/Aquincum" title="Aquincum">Aquincum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>), Bononia (<a href="/wiki/Vidin" title="Vidin">Vidin</a>, Bulgaria), Ulcisia Vetera, Castra Florentium, Intercisa (<a href="/wiki/Duna%C3%BAjv%C3%A1ros" title="Dunaújváros">Dunaújváros</a>, Hungary), and Onagrinum (<a href="/wiki/Bege%C4%8D" title="Begeč">Begeč</a>, Serbia). The new forts became part of a new defensive line called the <i>Ripa Sarmatica</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In 295 and 296 Diocletian campaigned in the region again, and won a victory over the Carpi in the summer of 296.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Later during both 299 and 302, as Diocletian was then residing in the East, it was Galerius' turn to campaign victoriously on the Danube.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> By the end of his reign, Diocletian had secured the entire length of the Danube, provided it with forts, bridgeheads, highways, and walled towns, and sent fifteen or more legions to patrol the region; an inscription at <a href="/wiki/Sexaginta_Prista" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexaginta Prista">Sexaginta Prista</a> on the Lower Danube extolled restored <i>tranquilitas</i> to the region.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The defense came at a heavy cost, but was a significant achievement in an area difficult to defend.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Galerius, meanwhile, was engaged during 291–293 in disputes in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Egypt" title="Upper Egypt">Upper Egypt</a>, where he suppressed a regional uprising.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> He would return to Syria in 295 to fight the revanchist Persian empire.<sup id="cite_ref-BNSCE17_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNSCE17-140">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian's attempts to bring the Egyptian tax system in line with Imperial standards stirred discontent, and a revolt swept the region after Galerius' departure.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The usurper <a href="/wiki/Domitius_Domitianus" title="Domitius Domitianus">L. Domitius Domitianus</a> declared himself Augustus in July or August 297. Much of Egypt, including <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, recognized his rule.<sup id="cite_ref-BNSCE17_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNSCE17-140">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian moved into Egypt to suppress him, first putting down rebels in the <a href="/wiki/Thebaid" title="Thebaid">Thebaid</a> in the autumn of 297,<sup id="cite_ref-pymwoj_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pymwoj-132">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> then moving on to besiege Alexandria. Domitianus died in December 297,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> by which time Diocletian had secured control of the Egyptian countryside. Alexandria, however, whose defense was organized under Domitianus' former <i><a href="/wiki/Corrector#Roman_Antiquity" title="Corrector">corrector</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Achilleus_(emperor)" class="mw-redirect" title="Achilleus (emperor)">Aurelius Achilleus</a>, was to hold out until a later date, probably March 298.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bureaucratic affairs were completed during Diocletian's stay:<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> a census took place, and Alexandria, in punishment for its rebellion, lost the ability to mint independently.<sup id="cite_ref-mjhujr_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mjhujr-145">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian's reforms in the region, combined with those of <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a>, brought Egyptian administrative practices much closer to Roman standards.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian travelled south along the Nile the following summer, where he visited <a href="/wiki/Oxyrhynchus" title="Oxyrhynchus">Oxyrhynchus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elephantine" title="Elephantine">Elephantine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mjhujr_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mjhujr-145">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> In Nubia, he made peace with the <a href="/wiki/Nobatae" class="mw-redirect" title="Nobatae">Nobatae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blemmyes" title="Blemmyes">Blemmyes</a> tribes. Under the terms of the peace treaty Rome's borders moved north to <a href="/wiki/Philae" title="Philae">Philae</a> and the two tribes received an annual gold stipend. Diocletian left Africa quickly after the treaty, moving from Upper Egypt in September 298 to Syria in February 299. He met with Galerius in Mesopotamia.<sup id="cite_ref-BNCE1718_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNCE1718-131">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_with_Persia">War with Persia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: War with Persia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Roman_relations_with_the_Parthians_and_Sassanids" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman relations with the Parthians and Sassanids">Roman relations with the Parthians and Sassanids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Persian_Wars" title="Roman–Persian Wars">Roman–Persian Wars</a></div>
<h4><span id="Invasion.2C_counterinvasion"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasion,_counterinvasion">Invasion, counterinvasion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Invasion, counterinvasion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diocletianus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Diocletianus.jpg/220px-Diocletianus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Diocletianus.jpg/330px-Diocletianus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Diocletianus.jpg/440px-Diocletianus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1088" data-file-height="562" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diocletianus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Military issue coin of Diocletian</div></div></div>
<p>In 294, <a href="/wiki/Narseh" title="Narseh">Narseh</a>, a son of Shapur who had been passed over for the Sassanid succession, came to power in Persia. Narseh eliminated <a href="/wiki/Bahram_III" title="Bahram III">Bahram III</a>, a young man installed in the wake of Bahram II's death in 293.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> In early 294, Narseh sent Diocletian the customary package of gifts between the empires, and Diocletian responded with an exchange of ambassadors. Within Persia, however, Narseh was destroying every trace of his immediate predecessors from public monuments. He sought to identify himself with the warlike kings <a href="/wiki/Ardashir_I" title="Ardashir I">Ardashir</a> (r. 226–41) and <a href="/wiki/Shapur_I" title="Shapur I">Shapur I</a> (r. 241–72), who had defeated and imprisoned Emperor <a href="/wiki/Valerian_(emperor)" title="Valerian (emperor)">Valerian</a> (r. 253–260) following his failed invasion of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Narseh declared war on Rome in 295 or 296. He appears to have first invaded western Armenia, where he seized the lands delivered to Tiridates in the peace of 287.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Narseh moved south into Roman Mesopotamia in 297, where he inflicted a severe defeat on Galerius in the region between Carrhae (<a href="/wiki/Harran" title="Harran">Harran</a>, Turkey) and Callinicum (<a href="/wiki/Raqqa" title="Raqqa">Raqqa</a>, Syria)<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> (and thus, the historian <a href="/wiki/Fergus_Millar" title="Fergus Millar">Fergus Millar</a> notes, probably somewhere on the <a href="/wiki/Balikh_River" title="Balikh River">Balikh River</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian may or may not have been present at the battle,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> but he quickly divested himself of all responsibility. In a public ceremony at Antioch, the official version of events was clear: Galerius was responsible for the defeat; Diocletian was not. Diocletian publicly humiliated Galerius, forcing him to walk for a mile at the head of the Imperial caravan, still clad in the purple robes of the Emperor.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;Note 7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arch-of-Galerius-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Arch-of-Galerius-1.jpg/220px-Arch-of-Galerius-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Arch-of-Galerius-1.jpg/330px-Arch-of-Galerius-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Arch-of-Galerius-1.jpg/440px-Arch-of-Galerius-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="532" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arch-of-Galerius-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of <a href="/wiki/Galerius" title="Galerius">Galerius</a> attacking <a href="/wiki/Narseh" title="Narseh">Narseh</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Arch_and_Tomb_of_Galerius#Arch_of_Galerius" class="mw-redirect" title="Arch and Tomb of Galerius">Arch of Galerius</a> at <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, the city where Galerius carried out most of his administrative actions<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Galerius was reinforced, probably in the spring of 298, by a new contingent collected from the empire's Danubian holdings.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Narseh did not advance from Armenia and Mesopotamia, leaving Galerius to lead the offensive in 298 with an attack on northern Mesopotamia via Armenia.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;Note 8&#93;</a></sup> It is unclear if Diocletian was present to assist the campaign; he might have returned to Egypt or Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;Note 9&#93;</a></sup> Narseh retreated to Armenia to fight Galerius' force, to Narseh's disadvantage; the rugged Armenian terrain was favorable to Roman infantry, but not to Sassanid cavalry. In two battles, Galerius won major victories over Narseh. During the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Satala_(298)" title="Battle of Satala (298)">second encounter</a>, Roman forces seized Narseh's camp, his treasury, his harem, and his wife.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Galerius continued moving down the Tigris, and took the Persian capital Ctesiphon before returning to Roman territory along the Euphrates.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace_negotiations">Peace negotiations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Peace negotiations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Narseh sent an ambassador to Galerius to plead for the return of his wives and children in the course of the war, but Galerius dismissed him.<sup id="cite_ref-BNSCE18_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNSCE18-166">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Serious peace negotiations began in the spring of 299. The <i>magister memoriae</i> (secretary) of Diocletian and Galerius, Sicorius Probus, was sent to Narseh to present terms.<sup id="cite_ref-BNSCE18_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNSCE18-166">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> The conditions of the resulting <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Nisibis_(299)" title="Peace of Nisibis (299)">Peace of Nisibis</a> were heavy:<sup id="cite_ref-P293_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P293-167">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Armenia returned to Roman domination, with the fort of Ziatha as its border; <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_Iberia" class="mw-redirect" title="Caucasian Iberia">Caucasian Iberia</a> would pay allegiance to Rome under a Roman appointee; Nisibis, now under Roman rule, would become the sole conduit for trade between Persia and Rome; and Rome would exercise control over the five satrapies between the Tigris and Armenia: Ingilene, Sophanene (<a href="/wiki/Sophene" title="Sophene">Sophene</a>), Arzanene (<a href="/wiki/Aghdznik" class="mw-redirect" title="Aghdznik">Aghdznik</a>), <a href="/wiki/Corduene" title="Corduene">Corduene</a> (Carduene), and Zabdicene (near modern <a href="/wiki/Hakk%C3%A2ri" title="Hakkâri">Hakkâri</a>, Turkey). These regions included the passage of the Tigris through the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Taurus_Mountains" title="Anti-Taurus Mountains">Anti-Taurus</a> range; the <a href="/wiki/Bitlis" title="Bitlis">Bitlis</a> pass, the quickest southerly route into Persian Armenia; and access to the <a href="/wiki/Tur_Abdin" title="Tur Abdin">Tur Abdin</a> plateau.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A stretch of land containing the later strategic strongholds of Amida (<a href="/wiki/Diyarbak%C4%B1r" title="Diyarbakır">Diyarbakır</a>, Turkey) and <a href="/wiki/Bezabde" title="Bezabde">Bezabde</a> came under firm Roman military occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-M178_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M178-169">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> With these territories, Rome would have an advance station north of Ctesiphon, and would be able to slow any future advance of Persian forces through the region.<sup id="cite_ref-P293_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P293-167">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Many cities east of the Tigris came under Roman control, including <a href="/wiki/Tigranocerta" title="Tigranocerta">Tigranokert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siirt" title="Siirt">Saird</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silvan,_Diyarbak%C4%B1r" title="Silvan, Diyarbakır">Martyropolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bitlis" title="Bitlis">Balalesa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bakhchisaray" class="mw-redirect" title="Bakhchisaray">Moxos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duhok,_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Duhok, Iraq">Daudia</a>, and Arzan&#160;– though under what status is unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-M178_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M178-169">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> At the conclusion of the peace, Tiridates regained both his throne and the entirety of his ancestral claim.<sup id="cite_ref-BNSCE18_166-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNSCE18-166">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Rome secured a wide zone of cultural influence, which led to a wide diffusion of <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">Syriac Christianity</a> from a center at Nisibis in later decades, and the eventual Christianization of Armenia.<sup id="cite_ref-P293_167-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P293-167">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To strengthen the defence of the east in future wars Diocletian had a fortified road constructed at the southern border, were the empire bordered the Arabs, in the year 300.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-170">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> This road would remain in use for centuries but proved ineffective in defending the border as conventional armies couldn’t operate in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-170">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_persecutions">Religious persecutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Religious persecutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Diocletianic_Persecution" title="Diocletianic Persecution">Diocletianic Persecution</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_persecutions">Early persecutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Early persecutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the conclusion of the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Nisibis_(299)" title="Peace of Nisibis (299)">Peace of Nisibis</a>, Diocletian and Galerius returned to Syrian Antioch.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> At some time in 299, the emperors took part in a ceremony of <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a> in an attempt to predict the future. The <a href="/wiki/Haruspex" title="Haruspex">haruspices</a> were unable to read the entrails of the sacrificed animals and blamed Christians in the Imperial household. The emperors ordered all members of the court to perform a sacrifice to purify the palace.
