The emperors sent letters to the military command, demanding the entire army perform the required sacrifices or face discharge.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian was conservative in matters of religion, a man faithful to the traditional Roman pantheon and understanding of demands for religious purification,<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" class="mw-redirect" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">Eusebius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lactantius" title="Lactantius">Lactantius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine</a> state that it was <a href="/wiki/Galerius" title="Galerius">Galerius</a>, not Diocletian, who was the prime supporter of the purge, and its greatest beneficiary.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Galerius, even more devoted and passionate than Diocletian, saw political advantage in the politics of persecution. He was willing to break with a government policy of inaction on the issue.<sup id="cite_ref-BCE19_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BCE19-175">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Antioch was Diocletian's primary residence from 299 to 302, while Galerius swapped places with his Augustus on the Middle and Lower Danube.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian visited Egypt once, over the winter of 301–2, and issued a grain dole in Alexandria.<sup id="cite_ref-BCE19_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BCE19-175">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Following some public disputes with <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manicheans</a>, Diocletian ordered that the leading followers of <a href="/wiki/Mani_(prophet)" title="Mani (prophet)">Mani</a> be burnt alive along with their scriptures. In a 31 March 302 rescript from Alexandria, he declared that low-status Manicheans must be executed by the blade, and high-status Manicheans must be sent to work in the quarries of Proconnesus (<a href="/wiki/Marmara_Island" title="Marmara Island">Marmara Island</a>, Turkey) or the mines of <a href="/wiki/Khirbat_Faynan" title="Khirbat Faynan">Phaeno</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>. All Manichean property was to be seized and deposited in the imperial treasury.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian found much to be offended by in Manichean religion: its novelty, its alien origins, the way it corrupted the morals of the Roman race, and its inherent opposition to long-standing religious traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The reasons he disliked Manichaeanism was at least equally applicable to his next target, Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ChristPeterPaul.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/ChristPeterPaul.jpg/220px-ChristPeterPaul.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/ChristPeterPaul.jpg/330px-ChristPeterPaul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/ChristPeterPaul.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="420" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ChristPeterPaul.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Catacomb of Saints Marcellinus and Peter on the <a href="/wiki/Via_Labicana" title="Via Labicana">Via Labicana</a>. Christ between <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Peter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul</a>. To the sides are the martyrs Gorgonius, Peter, Marcellinus, <a href="/wiki/Tiburtius_and_Susanna" class="mw-redirect" title="Tiburtius and Susanna">Tiburtius</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Persecution">Great Persecution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Great Persecution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diocletian returned to Antioch in the autumn of 302. He ordered that the <a href="/wiki/Deacon" title="Deacon">deacon</a> <a href="/wiki/Romanus_of_Caesarea" title="Romanus of Caesarea">Romanus of Caesarea</a> have his tongue removed for defying the order of the courts and interrupting official sacrifices. Romanus was then sent to prison, where he was executed on 17 November 303. Diocletian believed that Romanus of Caesarea was arrogant, and he left the city for Nicomedia in the winter, accompanied by Galerius.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> According to Lactantius, Diocletian and Galerius entered into an argument over imperial policy towards Christians while wintering at Nicomedia in 302. Diocletian argued that forbidding Christians from the bureaucracy and military would be sufficient to appease the gods, but Galerius pushed for extermination. The two men sought the advice of the <a href="/wiki/Oracle" title="Oracle">oracle</a> of <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> at <a href="/wiki/Didyma" title="Didyma">Didyma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> The oracle responded that the impious on Earth hindered Apollo's ability to provide advice. Rhetorically Eusebius records the Oracle as saying "The just on Earth..."<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> These impious, Diocletian was informed by members of the court, could only refer to the Christians of the empire. At the behest of his court, Diocletian acceded to demands for universal persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 23 February 303, Diocletian ordered that the newly built church at Nicomedia be razed. He demanded that its scriptures be burned, and seized its precious stores for the treasury.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> The next day, Diocletian's first "Edict against the Christians" was published.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The edict ordered the destruction of Christian scriptures and places of worship across the empire, and prohibited Christians from assembling for worship.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Before the end of February, a fire destroyed part of the Imperial palace.<sup id="cite_ref-B24S168_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B24S168-188">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Galerius convinced Diocletian that the culprits were Christians, conspirators who had plotted with the <a href="/wiki/Eunuch_(court_official)" class="mw-redirect" title="Eunuch (court official)">eunuchs</a> of the palace. An investigation was commissioned, but no responsible party was found. Executions followed anyway, and the palace eunuchs Dorotheus and <a href="/wiki/Gorgonius" title="Gorgonius">Gorgonius</a> were executed. One individual, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Cubicularius" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter Cubicularius">Peter Cubicularius</a>, was stripped, raised high, and scourged. Salt and vinegar were poured in his wounds, and he was <a href="/wiki/Boiling_to_death" class="mw-redirect" title="Boiling to death">slowly boiled</a> over an open flame. The executions continued until at least 24 April 303, when six individuals, including the <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishop</a> <a href="/wiki/Anthimus_of_Nicomedia" title="Anthimus of Nicomedia">Anthimus</a>, were <a href="/wiki/Decapitation" title="Decapitation">decapitated</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BCE24_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BCE24-189">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> A second fire occurred sixteen days after the first. Galerius left the city for Rome, declaring Nicomedia unsafe.<sup id="cite_ref-B24S168_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B24S168-188">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian would soon follow.<sup id="cite_ref-BCE24_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BCE24-189">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although further persecutionary edicts followed, compelling the arrest of the Christian clergy and universal acts of sacrifice,<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> the persecutionary edicts were ultimately unsuccessful; most Christians escaped punishment, and pagans too were generally unsympathetic to the persecution. The <a href="/wiki/Martyr" title="Martyr">martyrs</a>' sufferings strengthened the resolve of their fellow Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Constantius and Maximian did not apply the later persecutionary edicts, and left the Christians of the West unharmed.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Galerius rescinded the edict in 311, announcing that the persecution had failed to bring Christians back to traditional religion.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The temporary apostasy of some Christians, and the surrendering of scriptures, during the persecution played a major role in the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Donatist" class="mw-redirect" title="Donatist">Donatist</a> controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Within twenty-five years of the persecution's inauguration, the Christian Emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine">Constantine</a> would rule the empire alone. He would reverse the consequences of the edicts, and return all confiscated property to Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Under Constantine's rule, Christianity would become the empire's preferred religion.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian was demonized by his Christian successors: <a href="/wiki/Lactantius" title="Lactantius">Lactantius</a> intimated that Diocletian's ascendancy heralded the apocalypse,<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Serbian_mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Serbian mythology">Serbian mythology</a>, Diocletian is remembered as <a href="/wiki/Dukljan" title="Dukljan">Dukljan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Devil" title="Devil">adversary</a> of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Later life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Illness_and_abdication">Illness and abdication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Illness and abdication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r923042769/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption-center{text-align:center;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:223px;max-width:223px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Diocletian%27s_Palace_%28original_appearance%29.jpg/219px-Diocletian%27s_Palace_%28original_appearance%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="219" height="135" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Diocletian%27s_Palace_%28original_appearance%29.jpg/329px-Diocletian%27s_Palace_%28original_appearance%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Diocletian%27s_Palace_%28original_appearance%29.jpg/438px-Diocletian%27s_Palace_%28original_appearance%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1599" data-file-height="985" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Reconstruction of the <a href="/wiki/Diocletian%27s_Palace" title="Diocletian&#39;s Palace">Palace of the Roman Emperor Diocletian</a> in its original appearance upon completion in 305, by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_H%C3%A9brard" title="Ernest Hébrard">Ernest Hébrard</a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:221px;max-width:221px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Diocletian%27s_Palace_from_the_air.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Diocletian%27s_Palace_from_the_air.jpg/219px-Diocletian%27s_Palace_from_the_air.jpg" decoding="async" width="219" height="162" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Diocletian%27s_Palace_from_the_air.jpg/329px-Diocletian%27s_Palace_from_the_air.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Diocletian%27s_Palace_from_the_air.jpg/438px-Diocletian%27s_Palace_from_the_air.jpg 2x" data-file-width="494" data-file-height="365" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Modern-day Diocletian's Palace (2012), as the core of the city of <a href="/wiki/Split,_Croatia" title="Split, Croatia">Split</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Diocletian entered the city of Rome in the early winter of 303. On 20 November, he celebrated, with Maximian, the twentieth anniversary of his reign (<i>vicennalia</i>), the tenth anniversary of the tetrarchy (<i><a href="/wiki/Decennalia" title="Decennalia">decennalia</a></i>), and a triumph for the war with Persia. Diocletian soon grew impatient with the city, as the Romans acted towards him with what <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a>, following <a href="/wiki/Lactantius" title="Lactantius">Lactantius</a>, calls "licentious familiarity".<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> The Roman people did not give enough deference to his supreme authority; it expected him to act the part of an aristocratic ruler, not a monarchic one. On 20 December 303,<sup id="cite_ref-tkuqen_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tkuqen-200">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian cut short his stay in Rome and left for the north. He did not even perform the ceremonies investing him with his ninth consulate; he did them in <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a> on 1 January 304 instead.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> There are suggestions in the <i><a href="/wiki/Panegyrici_Latini" title="Panegyrici Latini">Panegyrici Latini</a></i> and Lactantius' account that Diocletian arranged plans for his and Maximian's future retirement of power in Rome. Maximian, according to these accounts, swore to uphold Diocletian's plan in a ceremony in the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Jupiter_Optimus_Maximus" title="Temple of Jupiter Optimus Maximus">Temple of Jupiter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From Ravenna, Diocletian left for the Danube. There, possibly in Galerius' company, he took part in a campaign against the Carpi.<sup id="cite_ref-tkuqen_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tkuqen-200">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> He contracted a minor illness while on campaign, but his condition quickly worsened and he chose to travel in a <a href="/wiki/Litter_(vehicle)" title="Litter (vehicle)">litter</a>. In the late summer he left for Nicomedia. On 20 November 304, he appeared in public to dedicate the opening of the circus beside his palace. He collapsed soon after the ceremonies. Over the winter of 304–5 he kept within his palace at all times. Rumours alleging that Diocletian's death was merely being kept secret until Galerius could come to assume power spread through the city. On 13 December, it appeared that he had finally died. The city was sent into a mourning from which it recovered after public declarations that Diocletian was still alive. When Diocletian reappeared in public on 1 March 305, he was emaciated and barely recognizable.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Galerius arrived in the city later in March. According to Lactantius, he came armed with plans to reconstitute the tetrarchy, force Diocletian to step down, and fill the Imperial office with men compliant to his will. Through coercion and threats, he eventually convinced Diocletian to comply with his plan. Lactantius also claims that he had done the same to Maximian at Sirmium.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> On 1 May 305, Diocletian called an assembly of his generals, traditional companion troops, and representatives from distant legions. They met at the same hill, 5 kilometres (3.1&#160;mi) out of Nicomedia, where Diocletian had been proclaimed emperor. In front of a statue of Jupiter, his patron deity, Diocletian addressed the crowd. With tears in his eyes, he told them of his weakness, his need for rest, and his will to resign. He declared that he needed to pass the duty of empire on to someone stronger. He thus became the first Roman emperor to voluntarily abdicate his title.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most in the crowd believed they knew what would follow; <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine</a> and Maxentius, the only adult sons of reigning emperors, men who had long been preparing to succeed their fathers, would be granted the title of caesar. Constantine had travelled through Palestine at the right hand of Diocletian, and was present at the palace in Nicomedia in 303 and 305. It is likely that Maxentius received the same treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> In Lactantius' account, when Diocletian announced that he was to resign, the entire crowd turned to face Constantine.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> It was not to be: <a href="/wiki/Flavius_Valerius_Severus" class="mw-redirect" title="Flavius Valerius Severus">Severus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maximinus_II" title="Maximinus II">Maximinus</a> were declared caesars. Maximinus appeared and took Diocletian's robes. On the same day, Severus received his robes from Maximian in Milan. Constantius succeeded Maximian as augustus of the West, but Constantine and Maxentius were entirely ignored in the transition of power. This did not bode well for the future security of the tetrarchic system.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement_and_death">Retirement and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Retirement and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_Folks%27_History_of_Rome_illus342.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Young_Folks%27_History_of_Rome_illus342.png/220px-Young_Folks%27_History_of_Rome_illus342.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Young_Folks%27_History_of_Rome_illus342.png/330px-Young_Folks%27_History_of_Rome_illus342.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/50/Young_Folks%27_History_of_Rome_illus342.png 2x" data-file-width="347" data-file-height="345" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_Folks%27_History_of_Rome_illus342.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Modern depiction of Diocletian in retirement</div></div></div>
<p>Diocletian retired to his homeland, <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia_(Roman_province)" title="Dalmatia (Roman province)">Dalmatia</a>. He moved into the expansive <a href="/wiki/Diocletian%27s_Palace" title="Diocletian&#39;s Palace">Diocletian's Palace</a>, a heavily fortified compound located by the small town of Spalatum on the shores of the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic Sea</a>, and near the large provincial administrative center of <a href="/wiki/Salona" title="Salona">Salona</a>. The palace is preserved in great part to this day and forms the historic core of <a href="/wiki/Split,_Croatia" title="Split, Croatia">Split</a>, the second-largest city of modern <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>.
</p><p>Maximian retired to villas in <a href="/wiki/Campania" title="Campania">Campania</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lucania" title="Lucania">Lucania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Their homes were distant from political life, but Diocletian and Maximian were close enough to remain in regular contact with each other.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> Galerius assumed the consular <i>fasces</i> in 308 with Diocletian as his colleague. In the autumn of 308, Galerius again conferred with Diocletian at <a href="/wiki/Carnuntum" title="Carnuntum">Carnuntum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Petronell-Carnuntum" title="Petronell-Carnuntum">Petronell-Carnuntum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>). Diocletian and Maximian were both present on 11 November 308, to see Galerius appoint <a href="/wiki/Licinius" title="Licinius">Licinius</a> to be Augustus in place of Severus, who had died at the hands of Maxentius. He ordered Maximian, who had attempted to return to power after his retirement, to step down permanently. At Carnuntum people begged Diocletian to return to the throne, to resolve the conflicts that had arisen through Constantine's rise to power and Maxentius' usurpation.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian's reply: "If you could show the <a href="/wiki/Cabbage" title="Cabbage">cabbage</a> that I planted with my own hands to your emperor, he definitely wouldn't dare suggest that I replace the peace and happiness of this place with the storms of a never-satisfied greed."<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He lived on for four more years, spending his days in his palace gardens. He saw his tetrarchic system fail, torn by the selfish ambitions of his successors. He heard of Maximian's third claim to the throne, his forced suicide, and his <i><a href="/wiki/Damnatio_memoriae" title="Damnatio memoriae">damnatio memoriae</a></i>. In his own palace, statues and portraits of his former companion emperor were torn down and destroyed. Deep in despair and illness, Diocletian may have committed <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a>. He died on 3 December 312.<sup id="cite_ref-DEATH_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DEATH-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;Note 10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reforms">Reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tetrarchic_and_ideological">Tetrarchic and ideological</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Tetrarchic and ideological">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peristyle_of_Diocletian%27s_Palace,_Split_(11908116224).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Peristyle_of_Diocletian%27s_Palace%2C_Split_%2811908116224%29.jpg/220px-Peristyle_of_Diocletian%27s_Palace%2C_Split_%2811908116224%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Peristyle_of_Diocletian%27s_Palace%2C_Split_%2811908116224%29.jpg/330px-Peristyle_of_Diocletian%27s_Palace%2C_Split_%2811908116224%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Peristyle_of_Diocletian%27s_Palace%2C_Split_%2811908116224%29.jpg/440px-Peristyle_of_Diocletian%27s_Palace%2C_Split_%2811908116224%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4899" data-file-height="3245" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peristyle_of_Diocletian%27s_Palace,_Split_(11908116224).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Modern view of the <a href="/wiki/Peristyle" title="Peristyle">Peristyle</a> in <a href="/wiki/Diocletian%27s_Palace" title="Diocletian&#39;s Palace">Diocletian's Palace</a> (<a href="/wiki/Split_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Split (city)">Split</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Diocletian saw his work as that of a restorer, a figure of authority whose duty it was to return the empire to peace, to recreate stability and justice where barbarian hordes had destroyed it.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> He arrogated, regimented and centralized political authority on a massive scale. In his policies, he enforced an Imperial system of values on diverse and often unreceptive provincial audiences.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> In the Imperial propaganda from the period, recent history was perverted and minimized in the service of the theme of the tetrarchs as "restorers". Aurelian's achievements were ignored, the revolt of Carausius was backdated to the reign of Gallienus, and it was implied that the tetrarchs engineered Aurelian's defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Palmyrene_Empire" title="Palmyrene Empire">Palmyrenes</a>; the period between Gallienus and Diocletian was effectively erased. The history of the empire before the tetrarchy was portrayed as a time of civil war, savage despotism, and imperial collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-oqujbw_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oqujbw-217">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> In those inscriptions that bear their names, Diocletian and his companions are referred to as "restorers of the whole world",<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> men who succeeded in "defeating the nations of the barbarians, and confirming the tranquility of their world".<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian was written up as the "founder of eternal peace".<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> The theme of restoration was conjoined to an emphasis on the uniqueness and accomplishments of the tetrarchs themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-oqujbw_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oqujbw-217">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The cities where emperors lived frequently in this period—<a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arles" title="Arles">Arles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sirmium" title="Sirmium">Sirmium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serdica" class="mw-redirect" title="Serdica">Serdica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicomedia" title="Nicomedia">Nicomedia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>—were treated as alternate imperial seats, to the exclusion of Rome and its senatorial elite.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> A new style of ceremony was developed, emphasizing the distinction of the emperor from all other persons. The quasi-republican ideals of Augustus' <i><a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">primus inter pares</a></i> were abandoned for all but the tetrarchs themselves. Diocletian took to wearing a gold crown and jewels, and forbade the use of <a href="/wiki/Tyrian_purple" title="Tyrian purple">purple cloth</a> to all but the emperors.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> His subjects were required to prostrate themselves in his presence (<i>adoratio</i>); the most fortunate were allowed the privilege of kissing the hem of his robe (<i><a href="/wiki/Proskynesis" title="Proskynesis">proskynesis</a></i>, προσκύνησις).<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Circuses and basilicas were designed to keep the face of the emperor perpetually in view, and always in a seat of authority. The emperor became a figure of transcendent authority, a man beyond the grip of the masses.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> His every appearance was stage-managed.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> This style of presentation was not new—many of its elements were first seen in the reigns of Aurelian and Severus—but it was only under the tetrarchs that it was refined into an explicit system.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative">Administrative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Administrative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In keeping with his move from an ideology of republicanism to one of autocracy, Diocletian's council of advisers, his <i>consilium</i>, differed from those of earlier emperors. He destroyed the Augustan illusion of imperial government as a cooperative affair among emperor, army, and senate.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> In its place he established an effectively autocratic structure, a shift later epitomized in the institution's name: it would be called a <i><a href="/wiki/Consistorium" title="Consistorium">consistorium</a></i>, not a council.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;Note 11&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian regulated his court by distinguishing separate departments (<i>scrinia</i>) for different tasks.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> From this structure came the offices of different <i>magistri</i>, like the <i><a href="/wiki/Magister_officiorum" title="Magister officiorum">magister officiorum</a></i> ("Master of Offices"), and associated secretariats. These were men suited to dealing with petitions, requests, correspondence, legal affairs, and foreign embassies. Within his court Diocletian maintained a permanent body of legal advisers, men with significant influence on his re-ordering of juridical affairs. There were also two finance ministers, dealing with the separate bodies of the public treasury and the private domains of the emperor, and the praetorian prefect, the most significant person of the whole. Diocletian's reduction of the Praetorian Guards to the level of a simple city garrison for Rome lessened the military powers of the prefect – although a prefect like Asclepiodotus was still a trained general<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> – but the office retained much civil authority. The prefect kept a staff of hundreds and managed affairs in all segments of government: in taxation, administration, jurisprudence, and minor military commands, the praetorian prefect was often second only to the emperor himself.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Altogether, Diocletian effected a large increase in the number of bureaucrats at the government's command; Lactantius was to claim that there were now more men using tax money than there were paying it.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> The historian Warren Treadgold estimates that under Diocletian the number of men in the <a href="/wiki/Civil_service" title="Civil service">civil service</a> doubled from 15,000 to 30,000.<sup id="cite_ref-civil_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-civil-235">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> The classicist <a href="/wiki/Roger_S._Bagnall" title="Roger S. Bagnall">Roger S. Bagnall</a> estimated that there was one bureaucrat for every 5–10,000 people in Egypt based on 400 or 800 bureaucrats for 4 million inhabitants (no one knows the population of the province in 300 AD; Strabo 300 years earlier put it at 7.5 million, excluding Alexandria). (By comparison, the ratio in 12th-century <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> China was one bureaucrat for every 15,000 people.) Jones estimated 30,000 bureaucrats for an empire of 50–65 million inhabitants, which works out to approximately 1,667 or 2,167 inhabitants per imperial official as averages empire-wide. The actual numbers of officials and ratios per inhabitant varied, of course, per <a href="/wiki/Roman_diocese" title="Roman diocese">diocese</a> depending on the number of provinces and population within a diocese. Provincial and diocesan paid officials (there were unpaid supernumeraries) numbered about 13–15,000 based on their staff establishments as set by law. The other 50% were with the emperor(s) in his or their <i>comitatus</i>, with the praetorian prefects, or with the grain supply officials in the capital (later, the capitals, Rome and Constantinople), Alexandria, and Carthage and officials from the central offices located in the provinces.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To avoid the possibility of local usurpations,<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> to facilitate a more efficient collection of taxes and supplies, and to ease the enforcement of the law, Diocletian doubled the number of <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">provinces</a> from fifty to almost one hundred.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> The provinces were grouped into twelve <a href="/wiki/Roman_diocese" title="Roman diocese">dioceses</a>, each governed by an appointed official called a <i><a href="/wiki/Vicarius" title="Vicarius">vicarius</a></i>, or "deputy of the praetorian prefects".<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Some of the provincial divisions required revision, and were modified either soon after 293 or early in the fourth century.<sup id="cite_ref-hcwmwc_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hcwmwc-240">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> Rome herself (including her environs, as defined by a 100 miles (160&#160;km)-<a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> <a href="/wiki/Perimeter" title="Perimeter">perimeter</a> around the city itself) was not under the authority of the praetorian prefect, as she was to be administered by a city prefect of senatorial rank&#160;– the sole prestigious post with actual power reserved exclusively for senators, except for some governors in Italy with the titles of corrector and the proconsuls of Asia and Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> The dissemination of imperial law to the provinces was facilitated under Diocletian's reign, because Diocletian's reform of the Empire's provincial structure meant that there were now a greater number of governors (<i><a href="/wiki/Praeses" title="Praeses">praesides</a></i>) ruling over smaller regions and smaller populations.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian's reforms shifted the governors' main function to that of the presiding official in the lower courts:<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> whereas in the early Empire military and judicial functions were the function of the governor, and <a href="/wiki/Promagistrate" title="Promagistrate">procurators</a> had supervised taxation, under the new system <i>vicarii</i> and governors were responsible for justice and taxation, and a new class of <i><a href="/wiki/Dux" title="Dux">duces</a></i> ("<a href="/wiki/Duke" title="Duke">dukes</a>"), acting independently of the civil service, had military command.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> These dukes sometimes administered two or three of the new provinces created by Diocletian, and had forces ranging from two thousand to more than twenty thousand men.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> In addition to their roles as judges and tax collectors, governors were expected to maintain the postal service (<i><a href="/wiki/Cursus_publicus" title="Cursus publicus">cursus publicus</a></i>) and ensure that town councils fulfilled their duties.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This curtailment of governors' powers as the Emperors' representatives may have lessened the political dangers of an all-too-powerful class of Imperial delegates, but it also severely limited governors' ability to oppose local landed elites, especially those of senatorial status, which, although with reduced opportunities for office holding, retained wealth, social prestige, and personal connections,<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> particularly in relatively peaceful regions without a great military presence.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> On one occasion, Diocletian had to exhort a proconsul of Africa not to fear the consequences of treading on the toes of the local magnates of senatorial rank.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> If a governor of senatorial rank himself felt these pressures, one can imagine the difficulties faced by a mere <i>praeses</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> That accounts for the strained relationship between the central power and local elites: sometime during 303, an attempted military sedition in <a href="/wiki/Seleucia_Pieria" title="Seleucia Pieria">Seleucia Pieria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> made Diocletian to extract a bloody retribution on both cities by putting to death a number of their council members for failing their duties of keeping order in their jurisdiction.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal">Legal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Legal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Digesto_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Digesto_02.jpg/170px-Digesto_02.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Digesto_02.jpg/255px-Digesto_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Digesto_02.jpg/340px-Digesto_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="591" data-file-height="875" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Digesto_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1581 reprint of the <i>Digestorum</i> from <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Corpus Juris Civilis</a></i> (527–534). The <i>Corpus</i> drew on the codices of <a href="/wiki/Codex_Gregorianus" title="Codex Gregorianus">Gregorius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Codex_Hermogenianus" title="Codex Hermogenianus">Hermogenian</a>, drafted and published under Diocletian's reign.</div></div></div>
<p>As with most emperors, much of Diocletian's daily routine rotated around legal affairs—responding to appeals and petitions, and delivering decisions on disputed matters. Rescripts, authoritative interpretations issued by the emperor in response to demands from disputants in both public and private cases, were a common duty of second- and third-century emperors. Diocletian was awash in paperwork, and was nearly incapable of delegating his duties. It would have been seen as a dereliction of duty to ignore them. In the "nomadic" imperial courts of the later Empire, one can track the progress of the imperial retinue through the locations from whence particular rescripts were issued – the presence of the Emperor was what allowed the system to function.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> Whenever the imperial court would settle in one of the capitals, there was a glut in petitions, as in late 294 in Nicomedia, where Diocletian kept winter quarters.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Admittedly, Diocletian's praetorian prefects—Afranius Hannibalianus, Julius Asclepiodotus, and <a href="/wiki/Hermogenian" title="Hermogenian">Aurelius Hermogenianus</a>—aided in regulating the flow and presentation of such paperwork, but the deep legalism of Roman culture kept the workload heavy.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Emperors in the forty years preceding Diocletian's reign had not managed these duties so effectively, and their output in attested rescripts is low. Diocletian, by contrast, was prodigious in his affairs: there are around 1,200 rescripts in his name still surviving, and these probably represent only a small portion of the total issue.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> The sharp increase in the number of edicts and rescripts produced under Diocletian's rule has been read as evidence of an ongoing effort to realign the whole Empire on terms dictated by the imperial center.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the governance of the <a href="/wiki/Jurist" title="Jurist">jurists</a> Gregorius, Aurelius Arcadius Charisius, and Hermogenianus, the imperial government began issuing official books of <a href="/wiki/Precedent" title="Precedent">precedent</a>, collecting and listing all the rescripts that had been issued from the reign of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a> (r. 117–38) to the reign of Diocletian.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Codex_Gregorianus" title="Codex Gregorianus">Codex Gregorianus</a> includes rescripts up to 292, which the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Hermogenianus" title="Codex Hermogenianus">Codex Hermogenianus</a> updated with a comprehensive collection of rescripts issued by Diocletian in 293 and 294.<sup id="cite_ref-hcwmwc_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hcwmwc-240">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> Although the very act of codification was a radical innovation, given the precedent-based design of the Roman legal system,<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> the jurists were generally conservative, and constantly looked to past Roman practice and theory for guidance.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> They were probably given more free rein over their codes than the later compilers of the <i><a href="/wiki/Codex_Theodosianus" title="Codex Theodosianus">Codex Theodosianus</a></i> (438) and <i><a href="/wiki/Codex_Justinianeus" title="Codex Justinianeus">Codex Justinianeus</a></i> (529) would have. Gregorius and Hermogenianus' codices lack the rigid structuring of later codes,<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> and were not published in the name of the emperor, but in the names of their compilers.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Their official character, however, was clear in that both collections were subsequently acknowledged by courts as authoritative records of imperial legislation up to the date of their publication and regularly updated.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After Diocletian's reform of the provinces, governors were called <i>iudex</i>, or <a href="/wiki/Judge" title="Judge">judge</a>. The governor became responsible for his decisions first to his immediate superiors, as well as to the more distant office of the emperor.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> It was most likely at this time that judicial records became verbatim accounts of what was said in trial, making it easier to determine bias or improper conduct on the part of the governor. With these records and the Empire's universal right of <a href="/wiki/Appeal" title="Appeal">appeal</a>, Imperial authorities probably had a great deal of power to enforce behavior standards for their judges.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> In spite of Diocletian's attempts at reform, the provincial restructuring was far from clear, especially when citizens appealed the decisions of their governors. Proconsuls, for example, were often both judges of first instance and appeal, and the governors of some provinces took appellant cases from their neighbors. It soon became impossible to avoid taking some cases to the emperor for arbitration and judgment.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian's reign marks the end of the classical period of Roman law. Where Diocletian's system of rescripts shows an adherence to classical tradition, Constantine's law is full of Greek and eastern influences.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Late_Roman_army#Diocletian" title="Late Roman army">Late Roman army: Diocletian</a></div>
<p>It is archaeologically difficult to distinguish Diocletian's fortifications from those of his successors and predecessors. The <a href="/wiki/Devil%27s_Dykes" title="Devil&#39;s Dykes">Devil's Dykes</a>, for example, the Danubian earthworks traditionally attributed to Diocletian, cannot even be securely dated to a particular century. The most that can be said about built structures under Diocletian's reign is that he rebuilt and strengthened forts at the Upper Rhine frontier (where he followed the works built under <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius_Probus" class="mw-redirect" title="Marcus Aurelius Probus">Probus</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Constance" title="Lake Constance">Lake Constance</a>-<a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a> and the Rhine–<a href="/wiki/Iller" title="Iller">Iller</a>–Danube line),<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> on the Danube- where a new line of forts on the far side of the river, the <i>Ripa Sarmatica</i>, was added to older, rehabilitated fortresses<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> – in Egypt, and on the frontier with Persia. Beyond that, much discussion is speculative and reliant on the broad generalizations of written sources. Diocletian and the tetrarchs had no consistent plan for frontier advancement, and records of raids and forts built across the frontier are likely to indicate only temporary claims. The <i><a href="/wiki/Strata_Diocletiana" title="Strata Diocletiana">Strata Diocletiana</a></i>, built after the Persian Wars, which ran from the Euphrates North of Palmyra and South towards northeast Arabia in the general vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Bostra" class="mw-redirect" title="Bostra">Bostra</a>, is the classic Diocletianic frontier system, consisting of an outer road followed by tightly spaced forts – defensible hard-points manned by small garrisons – followed by further fortifications in the rear.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> In an attempt to resolve the difficulty and slowness of transmitting orders to the frontier, the new capitals of the tetrarchic era were all much closer to the empire's frontiers than Rome had been:<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> Trier sat on the <a href="/wiki/Moselle" title="Moselle">Moselle</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Tributary" title="Tributary">tributary</a> of the Rhine, Sirmium and Serdica were close to the Danube, Thessaloniki was on the route leading eastward, and Nicomedia and Antioch were important points in dealings with Persia.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lactantius criticized Diocletian for an excessive increase in troop sizes, declaring that "each of the four [tetrarchs] strove to have a far larger number of troops than previous emperors had when they were governing the state alone".<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> The fifth-century pagan <a href="/wiki/Zosimus" title="Zosimus">Zosimus</a>, by contrast, praised Diocletian for keeping troops on the borders, rather than keeping them in the cities, as Constantine was held to have done.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> Both these views had some truth to them, despite the biases of their authors: Diocletian and the tetrarchs did greatly expand the army, and the growth was mostly in frontier regions, where the increased effectiveness of the new Diocletianic legions seem to have been mostly spread across a network of strongholds.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, it is difficult to establish the precise details of these shifts given the weakness of the sources.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> The army expanded to about 580,000 men from a 285 strength of 390,000, of which 310,000 men were stationed in the East, most of whom manned the Persian frontier. The navy's forces increased from approximately 45,000 men to approximately 65,000 men.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;Note 12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diocletian's expansion of the army and civil service meant that the empire's tax burden grew. Since military upkeep took the largest portion of the imperial budget, any reforms here would be especially costly.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> The proportion of the adult male population, excluding slaves, serving in the army increased from roughly 1 in 25 to 1 in 15, an increase judged excessive by some modern commentators. Official troop allowances were kept to low levels, and the mass of troops often resorted to extortion or the taking of civilian jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-lpnxzn_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lpnxzn-281">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> Arrears became the norm for most troops. Many were even given payment in kind in place of their salaries.<sup id="cite_ref-SSC159_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSC159-282">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> Were he unable to pay for his enlarged army, there would likely be civil conflict, potentially open revolt. Diocletian was led to devise a new system of taxation.<sup id="cite_ref-lpnxzn_281-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lpnxzn-281">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic">Economic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Economic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation">Taxation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Taxation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the early empire (30 BC&#160;– AD 235) the Roman government paid for what it needed in gold and silver. The coinage was stable. Requisition, forced purchase, was used to supply armies on the march. During the third century crisis (235–285), the government resorted to requisition rather than payment in debased coinage, since it could never be sure of the value of money. Requisition was nothing more or less than seizure. Diocletian made requisition into tax. He introduced an extensive new tax system based on heads (<i>capita</i>) and land (<i>iugera</i>) – with one iugerum equal to approximately .65 acres – and tied to a new, regular census of the empire's population and wealth. Census officials traveled throughout the empire, assessed the value of labor and land for each landowner, and joined the landowners' totals together to make citywide totals of <i>capita</i> and <i>iuga</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-TBSS20_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TBSS20-283">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> The <i>iugum</i> was not a consistent measure of land, but varied according to the type of land and crop, and the amount of labor necessary for sustenance. The <i>caput</i> was not consistent either: women, for instance, were often valued at half a <i>caput</i>, and sometimes at other values.<sup id="cite_ref-SSC159_282-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSC159-282">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> Cities provided animals, money, and manpower in proportion to its <i>capita</i>, and grain in proportion to its <i>iuga</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-TBSS20_283-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TBSS20-283">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;Note 13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most taxes were due on each year on 1 September, and levied from individual landowners by <i><a href="/wiki/Decurion_(administrative)" class="mw-redirect" title="Decurion (administrative)">decuriones</a></i> (decurions). These decurions, analogous to city councilors, were responsible for paying from their own pocket what they failed to collect.<sup id="cite_ref-sfirim_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sfirim-286">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian's reforms also increased the number of financial officials in the provinces: more <i>rationales</i> and <i>magistri privatae</i> are attested under Diocletian's reign than before. These officials represented the interests of the fisc, which collected taxes in gold, and the Imperial properties.<sup id="cite_ref-hcwmwc_240-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hcwmwc-240">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> Fluctuations in the value of the currency made collection of taxes in kind the norm, although these could be converted into coin. Rates shifted to take inflation into account.<sup id="cite_ref-TBSS20_283-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TBSS20-283">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> In 296, Diocletian issued an edict reforming census procedures. This edict introduced a general five-year census for the whole empire, replacing prior censuses that had operated at different speeds throughout the empire. The new censuses would keep up with changes in the values of <i>capita</i> and <i>iuga</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Italy, which had long been exempt from taxes, was included in the tax system from 290/291 as a diocesis.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> The city of Rome itself, however, remained exempt; the "regions" (i.e., provinces) South of Rome (generally called "suburbicarian", as opposed to the Northern, "annonaria" region) seem to have been relatively less taxed, in what probably was a sop offered to the great senatorial families and their landed properties.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diocletian's edicts emphasized the common liability of all taxpayers. Public records of all taxes were made public.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> The position of <i>decurion</i>, member of the city council, had been an honor sought by wealthy aristocrats and the middle classes who displayed their wealth by paying for city amenities and public works. Decurions were made liable for any shortfall in the amount of tax collected. Many tried to find ways to escape the obligation.<sup id="cite_ref-sfirim_286-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sfirim-286">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> By 300, civilians across the empire complained that there were more tax collectors than there were people to pay taxes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown198925_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown198925-291">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Currency_and_inflation">Currency and inflation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Currency and inflation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edict_on_Maximum_Prices_Diocletian_piece_in_Berlin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Edict_on_Maximum_Prices_Diocletian_piece_in_Berlin.jpg/220px-Edict_on_Maximum_Prices_Diocletian_piece_in_Berlin.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Edict_on_Maximum_Prices_Diocletian_piece_in_Berlin.jpg/330px-Edict_on_Maximum_Prices_Diocletian_piece_in_Berlin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Edict_on_Maximum_Prices_Diocletian_piece_in_Berlin.jpg/440px-Edict_on_Maximum_Prices_Diocletian_piece_in_Berlin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1195" data-file-height="1035" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edict_on_Maximum_Prices_Diocletian_piece_in_Berlin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A fragment of the <i><a href="/wiki/Edict_on_Maximum_Prices" title="Edict on Maximum Prices">Edict on Maximum Prices</a></i> (301), on display in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Prices_edict_Greek.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Prices_edict_Greek.jpg/220px-Prices_edict_Greek.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Prices_edict_Greek.jpg/330px-Prices_edict_Greek.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Prices_edict_Greek.jpg/440px-Prices_edict_Greek.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1098" data-file-height="975" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Prices_edict_Greek.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Part of the prices edict in Greek in its original area built into a medieval church, Geraki, Greece</div></div></div>
<p>Aurelian's attempt to reform the currency had failed; the denarius was dead.<sup id="cite_ref-SSC160_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSC160-292">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian restored the three-metal coinage and issued better quality pieces.<sup id="cite_ref-POTBAY392_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-POTBAY392-293">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> The new system consisted of five coins: the <i>aureus</i>/<i><a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)">solidus</a></i>, a gold coin weighing, like its predecessors, one-sixtieth of a pound; the <i><a href="/wiki/Argenteus" title="Argenteus">argenteus</a></i>, a coin weighing one ninety-sixth of a pound and containing ninety-five percent pure silver; the <i><a href="/wiki/Follis" title="Follis">follis</a></i>, sometimes referred to as the <i>laureatus</i> A, which is a copper coin with added silver struck at the rate of thirty-two to the pound; the <i>radiatus</i>, a small copper coin struck at the rate of 108 to the pound, with no added silver; and a coin known today as the <i>laureatus</i> B, a smaller copper coin struck at the rate of 192 to the pound.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;Note 14&#93;</a></sup> Since the nominal values of these new issues were lower than their intrinsic worth as metals, the state was minting these coins at a loss. This practice could be sustained only by requisitioning precious metals from private citizens in exchange for state-minted coin (of a far lower value than the price of the precious metals requisitioned).<sup id="cite_ref-CAS76_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CAS76-296">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 301, however, the system was in trouble, strained by a new bout of inflation. Diocletian therefore issued his <i>Edict on Coinage</i>, an act re-tariffing all debts so that the <i><a href="/wiki/Nummus" title="Nummus">nummus</a></i>, the most common coin in circulation, would be worth half as much.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> In the edict, preserved in an inscription from the city of <a href="/wiki/Aphrodisias" title="Aphrodisias">Aphrodisias</a> in <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a> (near <a href="/wiki/Geyre" title="Geyre">Geyre</a>, Turkey), it was declared that all debts contracted before 1 September 301 must be repaid at the old standards, while all debts contracted after that date would be repaid at the new standards.<sup id="cite_ref-POTBAY33435_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-POTBAY33435-298">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> It appears that the edict was made in an attempt to preserve the current price of gold and to keep the Empire's coinage on silver, Rome's traditional metal currency.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> This edict risked giving further momentum to inflationary trends, as had happened after Aurelian's currency reforms. The government's response was to issue a price freeze.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Edict_on_Maximum_Prices" title="Edict on Maximum Prices">Edict on Maximum Prices</a></i> (<i>Edictum De Pretiis Rerum Venalium</i>) was issued two to three months after the coinage edict,<sup id="cite_ref-SSC160_292-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSC160-292">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> somewhere between 20 November and 10 December 301.<sup id="cite_ref-POTBAY33435_298-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-POTBAY33435-298">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> The best-preserved Latin inscription surviving from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_East" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek East">Greek East</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> the edict survives in many versions, on materials as varied as wood, papyrus, and stone.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> In the edict, Diocletian declared that the current pricing crisis resulted from the unchecked greed of merchants, and had resulted in turmoil for the mass of common citizens. The language of the edict calls on the people's memory of their benevolent leaders, and exhorts them to enforce the provisions of the edict, and thereby restore perfection to the world. The edict goes on to list in detail over one thousand goods and accompanying retail prices not to be exceeded. Penalties are laid out for various pricing transgressions.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the most basic terms, the edict was ignorant of the law of <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply and demand</a>: it ignored the fact that prices might vary from region to region according to product availability, and it ignored the impact of transportation costs in the retail price of goods. In the judgment of the historian David Potter, the edict was "an act of economic lunacy".<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> The fact that the edict began with a long rhetorical preamble betrays at the same time a moralizing stance as well as a weak grasp of economics – perhaps simply the wishful thinking that criminalizing a practice was enough to stop it.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is no consensus about how effectively the edict was enforced.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> Supposedly, inflation, speculation, and monetary instability continued, and a black market arose to trade in goods forced out of official markets.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> The edict's penalties were applied unevenly across the empire (some scholars believe they were applied only in Diocletian's domains),<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> widely resisted, and eventually dropped, perhaps within a year of the edict's issue.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> Lactantius has written of the perverse accompaniments to the edict; of goods withdrawn from the market, of brawls over minute variations in price, of the deaths that came when its provisions were enforced. His account may be true, but it seems to modern historians exaggerated and hyperbolic,<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> and the impact of the law is recorded in no other ancient source.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_professional_mobility">Social and professional mobility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Social and professional mobility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Partly in response to economic pressures and in order to protect the vital functions of the state, Diocletian restricted social and professional mobility. Peasants became tied to the land in a way that presaged later systems of land tenure and workers such as bakers, armourers, public entertainers and workers in the mint had their occupations made hereditary.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> Soldiers' children were also forcibly enrolled, something that followed spontaneous tendencies among the rank-and-file, but also expressed increasing difficulties in recruitment.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The historian <a href="/wiki/A.H.M._Jones" class="mw-redirect" title="A.H.M. Jones">A.H.M. Jones</a> observed that "It is perhaps Diocletian's greatest achievement that he reigned twenty-one years and then abdicated voluntarily, and spent the remaining years of his life in peaceful retirement."<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian was one of the few emperors of the third and fourth centuries to die naturally, and the first in the history of the empire to retire voluntarily.<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> Once he retired, however, his tetrarchic system collapsed. Without the guiding hand of Diocletian, the empire fell into civil wars. Stability emerged after the defeat of Licinius by Constantine in 324.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> Under the Christian Constantine, Diocletian was maligned. Constantine's rule, however, validated Diocletian's achievements and the autocratic principle he represented:<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> the borders remained secure, in spite of Constantine's large expenditure of forces during his civil wars; the bureaucratic transformation of Roman government was completed; and Constantine took Diocletian's court ceremonies and made them even more extravagant.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Constantine ignored those parts of Diocletian's rule that did not suit him. Diocletian's policy of preserving a stable silver coinage was abandoned, and the gold <i><a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)">solidus</a></i> became the empire's primary currency instead.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> Diocletian's <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians" title="Persecution of Christians">persecution of Christians</a> was repudiated and changed to a policy of toleration and then favoritism. Christianity eventually became the official religion in 380. Most importantly, Diocletian's tax system and administrative reforms lasted, with some modifications, until the advent of the Muslims in the 630s.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup> The combination of state autocracy and state religion was instilled in much of Europe, particularly in the lands which adopted Orthodox Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to his administrative and legal impact on history, the Emperor Diocletian is considered to be the founder of the city of <a href="/wiki/Split,_Croatia" title="Split, Croatia">Split</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>. The city itself grew around the heavily fortified <a href="/wiki/Diocletian%27s_Palace" title="Diocletian&#39;s Palace">Diocletian's Palace</a> the emperor had built in anticipation of his retirement.
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Era_of_Martyrs" class="mw-redirect" title="Era of Martyrs">Era of Martyrs</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i>anno martyrum</i> or AM), also known as the <i>Diocletian era</i> (Latin: <i>anno Diocletiani</i>), is a method of numbering years used by the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Alexandria" title="Church of Alexandria">Church of Alexandria</a> beginning in the 4th century <i>anno Domini</i> and by the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria</a> from the 5th century to the present. In this system of counting, the beginning of Diocletian's reign in 284 was used as the <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(reference_date)" class="mw-redirect" title="Epoch (reference date)">epoch</a>, making Diocletian's first year in power into the Year 1 of that calendar. Western Christians were aware of this count but did not use it; <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_Exiguus" title="Dionysius Exiguus">Dionysius Exiguus</a> replaced the anno Diocletiani era with his <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">anno Domini</a> era because he did not wish to continue the memory of a tyrant who persecuted Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> The anno Domini era became dominant in the Latin West but was not used in the Greek East until modern times.
</p>
