<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diocletian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Coins are issued in his name in <a href="/wiki/Cyzicus" title="Cyzicus">Cyzicus</a> at some time before the end of 284, but it is impossible to know whether he was still in the public eye by that point.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Barnes and Bowman argue for 21 July,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Potter for 25 July.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He is placed there by a rescript dated 3 March 286.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He is attested there in a rescript dated 31 May 287.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The Jewish Midrash suggests that Diocletian resided at Panias (present-day Banias) in the northern Golan Heights.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-96">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The chronology of Maximian's appointment as augustus is somewhat uncertain.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Some suggest that Maximian was appointed augustus from the beginning of his imperial career, without ever holding the office of caesar;<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> others date the assumption of the Augustan title to 1 March 286.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> 1 April 286 is the most common date used in modern histories of the period.<sup id="cite_ref-AUGGGG_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AUGGGG-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-122">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The suggested dates for Galerius' appointment are 1 March and 21 May. There is no consensus on which is correct.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-155"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-155">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It is possible that Galerius' position at the head of the caravan was merely the conventional organization of an imperial progression, designed to show a caesar's deference to his augustus, and not an attempt to humiliate him.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-160"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-160">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Faustus_of_Byzantium" title="Faustus of Byzantium">Faustus of Byzantium</a>'s history refers to a battle that took place after Galerius set up base at <a href="/wiki/Satala" title="Satala">Satala</a> (Sadak, Turkey) in <a href="/wiki/Armenia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Armenia Minor">Armenia Minor</a>, when Narseh advanced from his base at Oskha to attack him.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Other histories of the period do not note these events.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-163"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-163">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lactantius criticizes Diocletian for his absence from the front,<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> but Southern, dating Diocletian's African campaigns one year earlier than Barnes, places Diocletian on Galerius' southern flank.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-214"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-214">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The range of dates proposed for Diocletian's death have stretched from 311 through to 318. Until recently, the date of 3 December 311 has been favoured; however, the absence of Diocletian on Maxentius' "<i>AETERNA MEMORIA</i>" coins would indicate that he was alive through to Maxentius' defeat in October 312. Given that Diocletian had died by the time of Maximin Daia's death in July 313, it has been argued that the correct date of death was 3 December 312.<sup id="cite_ref-DEATH_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DEATH-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-230"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-230">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The term <i>consistorium</i> was already in use for the room where council meetings took place.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-279"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-279">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Byzantine author <a href="/wiki/Joannes_Laurentius_Lydus" class="mw-redirect" title="Joannes Laurentius Lydus">John Lydus</a> provides extraordinarily precise troop numbers: 389,704 in the army and 45,562 in the navy.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> His precision has polarized modern historians. Some believe that Lydus found these figures in official documents and that they are therefore broadly accurate; others believe that he fabricated them.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-285"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-285">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The army recruitment tax was called the <i>praebitio tironum</i>, and conscripted a part of each landowner's tenant farmers (<i><a href="/wiki/Colonus_(person)" title="Colonus (person)">coloni</a></i>). When a <i>capitulum</i> extended across many farms, farmers provided the funds to compensate the neighbor who had supplied the recruit. Landowners of senatorial rank were able to commute the tax with a payment in gold (the <i>aurum tironicum</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-295"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-295">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i><a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a></i> was dropped from the Imperial mints,<sup id="cite_ref-SSC160_292-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSC160-292">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> but the values of new coins continued to be measured in reference to it.<sup id="cite_ref-POTBAY392_293-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-POTBAY392-293">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Williams, 36.</span>

<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Theodor Mommsen, <i>A History of Rome Under the Emperors</i>. London: Routledge, 1999, page 348. Mommsen offers a general remark on the political history of the Third Century Rome: "Those accounts we do possess stem from outsiders who in fact know nothing" – 346. A modern historian like Jill Harries, <i>Imperial Rome AD 284 to 363: The New Empire</i>, Edinburgh University Press, 2012, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-7486-2052-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-7486-2052-4">978-0-7486-2052-4</a>, page 27, calls Carus's death account, outrightly, a "story".</span>
